1594201927|3.0|Wow, can Niall Ferguson write like an angel.  Ascent of Money really carries you along, combining great stories with nitty-gritty financial details.  It's even more impressive given that Ferguson seems to churn out at least one book and several articles every year: maybe he doesn't need to sleep.<br /><br />Or maybe he needs to think a little more deeply.  The Ascent of Money is full of fascinating details, and if you don't know anything about finance, it's incredibly informative.  Ferguson probably thinks you DON'T know anything about finance: his first chapter concerns how little Americans do know about it.<br /><br />But if you want to go deeper, then there's not much here.  Ferguson teaches financial history at Harvard, and it's not too much to suspect that he is lifting from his lecture notes, which are interesting but not really substantive.<br /><br />At times, his ideological biases get the better of him.  Ferguson appears to be something of a conservative in this book, and he advised John McCain in the 2008 Presidential campaign.  Fair enough.  But when he takes on progressive thinking, he really falls short.  For example, he notes that east Asian economies grew rapidly after 2000, and thus concludes that Paul Krugman was wrong in his prediction of the return of depressions.  But Krugman didn't quite say that: he said that the growth of international capital flows eroded the \\"Keynesian Compact,\\" making severe recessions more likely.  The point was that we have returned to Depression ECONOMICS, not the Great Depression.<br /><br />Similarly, he dismisses Joseph Stiglitz' critique of the IMF by quoting Kenneth Rogoff's infamous cheap shot \\"open letter\\", which itself distorted Stiglitz' views.  Rogoff ridiculed Stiglitz for allegedly saying that developing countries should run up deficits if they were facing inflationary pressures.  He said nothing of the kind: rather, Stiglitz said that the IMF was forcing developing countries to deflate and bring on recessions when their only problem concerned international monetary speculations.  Many countries forced to austerity had balanced budgets.  And he makes the elementary mistake of claiming that US entitlement programs face collapse by conflating Social Security and Medicare.<br /><br />So you should read this book if you want an excellent and at times bracing narrative of the history of many financial institutions.  The accounts of the Rothschilds and the Napoleonic Wars are alone close to the price of admission.  Few, if any, scholars have the range of Ferguson.  And you'll learn something.  But look at books like the Trillion Dollar Meltdown, or, yes, The Return of Depression Economics if you really want to have an understanding of the current crisis.

0316067601|5.0|If you enjoy stories about Navy SEALs, Special Ops, or just small unit fighting, this is a very worthwhile read.  I'm a spec warfare junky so I've read hundreds of books around the subject.  This is near the top.    The author's description of BUD/S training and hell week are consistent with other accounts.  Despite having read the same general review of SEAL training in other books countless times, his description did not come off as boring or repetitive.  Perhaps it's the extreme nature of the training that always seems to make it interesting but I have to give the author credit for making it personal.    Where this book really sets itself apart from other combat books is the telling of his team's ordeal in the Hindu Kush mountains.  Obviously, the account is unique and only he can tell it, but it goes beyond that.  Mr. Luttrell had me riveted and by the end of the book, quite emotional.  I don't ever get emotional reading a book but this one got to me.    The writing style is interesting.  It's a little choppy -- the writing jumps between thoughts and occasionally gets sidelined with commentary.  However, that's appealing to me in that it seems to be more of the authors direct thoughts and words as opposed to a being a 'scrubbed' tale made more dramatic by a professional writer.  It would be hard to make this account much more dramatic.  Frankly, I can't imagine how Mr. Luttrell survived.    As a warning, Mr. Luttrell does make his political feelings known.  I appreciate that he doesn't go beyond his immediate area of expertise (i.e. he comments on the politicians impact on soldiers and our ability to wage war effectively).   I generally don't like political commentary mixed into these accounts but in this case, I couldn't agree with Mr. Luttrell more.  I'm glad someone is saying it -- particularly someone with as much credibility and first hand knowledge as the author.      As an aside... 0887308589|4.0|The \\"Peter Principle\\" says that competent people are promoted until they reach a job they can't handle, where they stop.  As a result, we are surrounded by once-competent but now-incompetent people (including ourselves).  The Dilbert Principle provides a less optimistic view of organizations: we promote incompetent people to get them away from where the real work is done.  As a result, we are ruled by incompetent managers, who continue to be promoted, and who earn large salaries and stock options.  Competent people at the bottom have to figure out a way to make the organization work, despite management.<br /><br />This book gives us a bunch of Scott Adams' \\"Dilbert Cartoons,\\" stories, short essays, and emails from fans, organized into a bunch of chapters around \\"Business Communication,\\" \\"Performance Reviews,\\" and \\"Swearing.\\"  It's funny, but choppy.<br /><br />Lurking behind all the fun is a serious critique of modern business management.  Adams draws this out in a brief conclusion, where he (seriously) lays out how he'd run a company.  There is Much To Be Learned here.<br /><br />Historians of the future will be shocked by it all.

B001EO748M|3.0|Showtime's second season of the historical soap opera \\"The Tudors\\" is a spectacular sequel to its popular and award-winning first. While season one flashed forward through ten years, the season two stretches out four years worth of history when Henry VIII divorced Katherine of Aragon, married and executed Anne Boleyn, and became engaged to Jane Seymour. The season begins in 1532 before Henry VIII reaches a break through in his \\"Great Matter\\", the annulment of his first marriage to Katherine which had stagnated for six years under the consideration of Pope Clement VII. This season brings new beginnings - new cast members, new characters, new plot lines, all culminating into a tragic, bloody and thrilling conclusion when the season ends with the execution of Anne Boleyn - May 19, 1536. It's a juicy drama adapted from enthralling history.    The second season is predominantly concerned with the rise and fall of Anne Boleyn but also the rise the Anglican schism from the Roman Catholic Church. In this season, Henry VIII turns from a monarch devoted to defending the Church from the likes of Martin Luther to a theologically flexible tyrant. Although the series as a whole gets numerous justified criticisms for propagating historical inaccuracies, most of what the series touches upon regarding the dissolution of the monasteries, the role of the King in the English Church, the pervasive and divisive role religion played in Tudor England is true and very powerfully portrayed. This series and any other drama or historical fiction should never be mistaken for a documentary and \\"The Tudors\\" has never been advertised by its creators as an entirely accurate work of historical scholarship. Therefore, if seeing a man who is brunette, 5'9\\", 31 years old, skinny, with lack of sufficient facial hair playing Henry VIII emotionally disturbs you, than you are probably better off not watching this series at all. (see instead: Keith Michell in \\"[[ASIN:B000EBD9VM The Six Wives of Henry VIII]]\\" or Robert Shaw in \\"[[ASIN:B000LPR6GA A Man for All Seasons (Special Edition)]]\\"). But if you can suspend disbelief and just take \\"The Tudors\\" for what it is, good entertainment, then you will find much to enjoy.    The DVD set of the second season itself, like the first season, disappoints. The special features are meager - two three minute featurettes, one about modern day descendants of Henry VIII which was more random than informative, and one mini documentary of Natalie Dormer visiting the Tower of London and revisiting the last days of Anne Boleyn which was good but has been available on Showtime's website since the season finale aired. There's also cast biographies, photo gallery, free season premiere episodes of Californication and This American Life, as well as additional features which you require internet access to view (premiere of season 3 of Dexter and two episodes of United States of Tara). Other than a Spanish dub there are no additional language or subtitle features, unless your DVD player is equipped for closed captioning there are no subtitles even in English. I would have liked more special features and language/subtitle options and I don't want to have to get on the internet to access them. Some cast or director commentaries would be a major improvement and maybe even an optional historical commentary feature like HBO has for its miniseries [[ASIN:B000WGWQG8 John Adams (HBO Miniseries)]] and [[ASIN:B000FJH4X2 Rome - The Complete First Season]] . \\"The Tudors\\" is presented in widescreen on four discs, unrated but contains nudity, violence, and explicit language. The second season itself gets four stars, but the poor features in the DVD set knocks off one star - THREE STARS.

B001EOQWEO|5.0|Wall-E in parts:    Musicals  If you know me, you'll know that I hate musicals - mostly, not entirely, but musicals are basically not my thing.  The first time I saw Wall-E, was the week the DVD became available for sale.  I promptly bought it (and subsequently bought the 3-disc package through Amazon), because so far I had loved all Pixar product released (I have some issues with Incredibles and Rata-tool, but I mostly love them also).  And, lo and behold, if my heart didn't sink at the beginning of the movie...I went a little cold, and started thinking that Pixar had gone the way of Disney animated films of the 80's and 90's - I thought that Wall-E was going to be adopting the musical format of the parent company.  But nothing could be further from the truth (thankfully).  The musicals and/or musical love/lover concept was injected into the story in the best most imaginative way I could never have imagined.    Story Arc  The story is the reason why I feel Wall-E should have been considered for an Oscar nomination - much more deservedly than Beauty and the Beast.  Because for me, a good story is when the main character's journey is really and truly well told, and when you can palpably see the evolution and or growth of one or more characters - one palpable moment is the scene where Wall-E is busted up pretty badly, he gives Eve the plant and says \\"Directive.\\"  Not only do you actually see Eve's growth and transformation into an emotional being, but Wall-E persists and reveals for her the grander and mor important ultimate \\"Directive\\" of going back to Earth.  I compare this with the growth and transformation of two sisters in Sense and Sensibility (Emma taking the cake with her virtual climax of emotion near the end), and Amy Adams in Enchanted when she convulsively realized that she is capable of feeling anger (that scene made the movie worthwhile for me).  Animation  Hayao Miyazaki is a master animation director and most of the Pixar directors are master animationdirectors (I will include John Kricfalusi, Tex Avery, Bob Clampett, and Hannah & Barbera to the master animation director category - I'm sure there are others, but their names escape me right now).  I believe that a sure-fire way to make fluid animation look crappy and ultimately turn out lousy is by doing a formula or format type of animation direction. I've come to loathe and practically convulse when watching characters make strange and unnecessary emotive motions, hand movements and gestures - if you know Futurama, it is probably the type of motion that Harold Zoid would actually like.  The joy of joys for me in animation, are moments like in Ren & Stimpy where a lot of their emoting is done mostly with their eyes, but then Stimpy manages to anger Ren and all hell breaks loose.  One might wonder why directors, if they truly want to take their craft seriously, can't see how the drama is intensified when things are mostly static and motionless until the moment is right and everything goes wild - or just the mere fact that if you were to see the same movements that you see in a lot of animation, translated to live action, you might think that there was some sort of weird Ed Wood revival of directing going on or something.  Bob movements.  I hate bob movements of meaningless, head, hands, arms, and body motion.  Andrew Stanton has just about perfected the elimination of bob movements in animation with his work in Wall-E (the same can not be said for the Rat-a-tool guy).    Animation studios  Of course, I don't hate everything that comes out of Dreamworks animation, but as far as I'm concerned, Katzenberg is heavily involved with a lot of what I hate in modern animation.  I'm talking about formulas and forcing every single gwad-lovin' animation feature idea into a musical format...did I mention that I hate musicals (for the most part)?  It doesn't stop there though.  There's something about using or misusing pop-references (in all movies, not just animation).  The way that a lot of studios use pop-references (including Disney and Dreamworks) pretty much guarantees that the particular film will become dated, and not a timeless classic.  And not only does the film become dated, but I hate that feeling when I get into a movie and try to loose myself in the film's fantasy, but all of a sudden I get the cold-water-splash of a pop-reference that lets me know it's only a movie.  I mean, how can I loose myself in a film, when the characters start quoting popular movies or making reference to the Terminator or something similar.  To me that's like the pin-prick popping the balloon.  There's hope though, I think, because I feel the Madagascar movies and Kung Fu Panda had some great stuff in them.  I pretty much hate all their other stuff.  It really broke my heart to see all those beautifully designed Aardman characters move in those god-awful bob movements that Nick Park would never do...well, I guess I would add Chicken Run and Wallace and Gromit: The Curse of the Were-Rabbit to the love category, although I don't strictly consider those 2 films as Dreamworks movies.  Plus, add to the \\"there's hope\\" in animation, the movie Happy Feet from Warner Bro's.  A moment to shed a single tear for the death of trad/drawn feature animation in the U.S....  But anyway, in my opinion, Wall-E re-certified Pixar, as the top feature animation studio in the U.S.

A neglected husband (or even a neglected wife) can easily stray towards someone who also needs some temporary affection. And, if a man's got money, there's no reason to resist temptation. I've heard it said: \\"A man is only as loyal as his options.\\"<br /><br />For the age of these women who make up the star cast, even Bette Midler, Carrie Fisher, and Candice Bergen aren't all that much older than the main cast. If they needed an older woman in here for some kind of elderly influence, they really needed to seek out a silver screen dinosaur. That's where we get Cloris Leachman. For all we know, she could have been in the original 1939 version.<br /><br />This movie's appeal is right there between \\"Designing Women\\" and \\"The Golden Girls\\". B00003JRCQ|5.0|The BBC/A&E production of Jane Austin's Persuation is a masterpiece (perhaps the reason it was shown in America by Masterpiece Theater) that should not be overlooked or left unseen.  Persuation is one of Austin's best works, surpassed only by her Pride and Prejudice (her coup de grace of any idea that only men could write great literature).  Unlike Pride and Prejudice, Persuation starts a little slow, which may cause viewers to think about turning it off -- Don't!  The ending is well worth the early wait, which establishes the setting and circumstances that the latter romantic comedy, the play of misunderstanding and morals depends.  Stay the course -- it will reward you well enough that you will return and watch Austin's work again and again.
